			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Ellen Johnson Sirleaf</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">

<p><b>Ellen Johnson Sirleaf</b> (born 29 October 1938) is a Liberian politician who serves as the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Liberia#List_of_Presidents_of_Liberia" title="President of Liberia">24th and current</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_Liberia" title="President of Liberia">President</a> of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> since 2006. Sirleaf is the first elected <a href="/wiki/List_of_elected_and_appointed_female_heads_of_state" class="mw-redirect" title="List of elected and appointed female heads of state">female head of state</a> in Africa.</p>
<p>Sirleaf was born in <a href="/wiki/Monrovia" title="Monrovia">Monrovia</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Gola_people" title="Gola people">Gola</a> father and <a href="/wiki/Kru_people" title="Kru people">Kru</a>-<a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German</a> mother. She was educated at the <a href="/wiki/College_of_West_Africa" title="College of West Africa">College of West Africa</a> before moving to the United States, where she studied at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Business_College" title="Madison Business College">Madison Business College</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>. She returned to Liberia to work in <a href="/wiki/William_Tolbert" class="mw-redirect" title="William Tolbert">William Tolbert</a>'s government as Assistant Minister of Finance from 1973–74 and Minister of Finance from 1979–80. After <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Doe" title="Samuel Doe">Samuel Doe</a> seized power in a coup d'état and executed Tolbert, Sirleaf fled to the U.S. She worked for the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> before moving to <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>, where she worked for <a href="/wiki/Citibank" title="Citibank">Citibank</a> and then the Equator Bank.</p>
<p>After returning to Liberia, Sirleaf ran for office, placing second in the <a href="/wiki/Liberian_presidential_election,_1997" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberian presidential election, 1997">1997 presidential election</a> won by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taylor_(Liberian_politician)" title="Charles Taylor (Liberian politician)">Charles Taylor</a>. She won the <a href="/wiki/Liberian_general_election,_2005" title="Liberian general election, 2005">2005 presidential election</a> and took office on 16 January 2006. She was <a href="/wiki/Liberian_general_election,_2011" title="Liberian general election, 2011">re-elected in 2011</a>. In June 2016, she was elected as the Chair of the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">Economic Community of West African States</a>, making her the first woman to occupy that position since it was formed.<sup id="cite_ref-GhanaStar_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GhanaStar-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, Sirleaf was jointly awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> with <a href="/wiki/Leymah_Gbowee" title="Leymah Gbowee">Leymah Gbowee</a> of Liberia and <a href="/wiki/Tawakkol_Karman" title="Tawakkol Karman">Tawakkol Karman</a> of <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>. The women were recognized "for their non-violent struggle for the safety of women and for women's rights to full participation in peace-building work."<sup id="cite_ref-nobel_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sirleaf was conferred the <a href="/wiki/Indira_Gandhi_Prize" title="Indira Gandhi Prize">Indira Gandhi Prize</a> by President of India <a href="/wiki/Pranab_Mukherjee" title="Pranab Mukherjee">Pranab Mukherjee</a> on 12 September 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-news.biharprabha.com_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.biharprabha.com-3">[3]</a></sup> As of 2016, she is listed as the <a href="/wiki/Forbes_list_of_The_World%27s_100_Most_Powerful_Women" title="Forbes list of The World's 100 Most Powerful Women">83rd-most powerful woman in the world</a> by <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes14_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes14-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_background">Family background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Family background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sirleaf's father was <a href="/wiki/Gola_people" title="Gola people">Gola</a> and her mother had mixed <a href="/wiki/Kru_people" title="Kru people">Kru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German</a> ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-brit_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-6">[6]</a></sup> While not <a href="/wiki/Americo-Liberian" class="mw-redirect" title="Americo-Liberian">Americo-Liberian</a> in terms of ancestry, because of her education in the West Sirleaf is considered culturally Americo-Liberian by some observers, or assumed to be Americo-Liberian.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Sirleaf does not identify as such.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sirleaf's father, Jahmale Carney Johnson, was born into a Gola family in an <a href="/wiki/Rural_poverty" title="Rural poverty">impoverished rural region</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-harv_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harv-11">[11]</a></sup> He was the son of a minor Gola chief named Jahmale and one of his wives, Jenneh, in Julijuah, <a href="/wiki/Bomi_County" title="Bomi County">Bomi County</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lib_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lib-12">[12]</a></sup> Her father was sent to <a href="/wiki/Monrovia" title="Monrovia">Monrovia</a>, where he changed his surname to Johnson due to his father's loyalty to President <a href="/wiki/Hilary_R._W._Johnson" title="Hilary R. W. Johnson">Hilary R. W. Johnson</a>, Liberia's first native-born president.<sup id="cite_ref-lib_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lib-12">[12]</a></sup> He grew up in Monrovia, where he was raised by an <a href="/wiki/Americo-Liberian" class="mw-redirect" title="Americo-Liberian">Americo-Liberian</a> family with the surname McCritty.<sup id="cite_ref-lib_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lib-12">[12]</a></sup> Sirleaf's father later became the first Liberian from an indigenous ethnic group to be elected to the country's national legislature.<sup id="cite_ref-brit_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-harv_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harv-11">[11]</a></sup> Sirleaf's mother was also born into poverty, in <a href="/wiki/Greenville,_Liberia" title="Greenville, Liberia">Greenville</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lib_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lib-12">[12]</a></sup> Her grandmother, Juah Sarwee, sent Sirleaf's mother to Monrovia when Sirleaf's German grandfather had to flee the country after Liberia declared war on Germany during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-harv_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harv-11">[11]</a></sup> A member of a prominent Americo-Liberian family, Cecilia Dunbar, adopted and raised Sirleaf's mother.<sup id="cite_ref-lib_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lib-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_career">Early life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sirleaf was born in <a href="/wiki/Monrovia" title="Monrovia">Monrovia</a> in 1938.<sup id="cite_ref-harv_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harv-11">[11]</a></sup> She attended the <a href="/wiki/College_of_West_Africa" title="College of West Africa">College of West Africa</a>, a preparatory school, from 1948 to 1955. She married James Sirleaf when she was 17 years old. They had four sons together, and she was primarily occupied as a homemaker. Early on in their marriage, James worked for the Department of Agriculture, and Sirleaf worked as a bookkeeper for an auto-repair shop.<sup id="cite_ref-AA_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AA-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>She traveled with James to the United States in 1961 to continue her studies and earned an associate degree in accounting at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Business_College" title="Madison Business College">Madison Business College</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Madison,_Wisconsin" title="Madison, Wisconsin">Madison, Wisconsin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bergner_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bergner-14">[14]</a></sup> When they returned to Liberia, James continued his work in the Agriculture Department and Sirleaf pursued a career in the Treasury Department (Ministry of Finance).<sup id="cite_ref-AA_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AA-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>They divorced in 1961 because of James' abuse.<sup id="cite_ref-brit_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AA_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AA-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sirleaf returned to college to complete her bachelor's degree. In 1970 she enrolled at the Economics Institute in <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">Boulder, Colorado</a>, where she spent the summer preparing for graduate studies. Sirleaf studied economics and public policy at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>'s <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_School_of_Government" title="John F. Kennedy School of Government">John F. Kennedy School of Government</a> from 1969 to 1971, gaining a <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Public_Administration" title="Master of Public Administration">Master of Public Administration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> She returned to her native Liberia to work in the administration of <a href="/wiki/William_Tolbert" class="mw-redirect" title="William Tolbert">William Tolbert</a>, where she was appointed as Assistant Minister of Finance. While in that position, she attracted attention with a "bombshell" speech to the Liberian Chamber of Commerce that claimed that the country's corporations were harming the economy by hoarding or sending their profits overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sirleaf served as assistant minister from 1972 to 1973 in the Tolbert administration. She resigned after a disagreement about government spending. Subsequently, she was appointed as Minister of Finance a few years later, serving from 1979 to April 1980.</p>
<p>Master Sergeant <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Doe" title="Samuel Doe">Samuel Doe</a>, a member of the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Krahn" class="mw-redirect" title="Krahn">Krahn</a> ethnic group, seized power in a 12 April 1980 <a href="/wiki/Military_coup" class="mw-redirect" title="Military coup">military coup</a>; he ordered the assassination of Tolbert and execution by firing squad of all but four members of his cabinet. The <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Redemption_Council" title="People's Redemption Council">People's Redemption Council</a> took control of the country and led a purge against the former government. Sirleaf initially accepted a post in the new government as President of the <a href="/wiki/Liberian_Bank_for_Development_and_Investment" title="Liberian Bank for Development and Investment">Liberian Bank for Development and Investment</a>. She fled the country in November 1980 after publicly criticizing Doe and the People's Redemption Council for their management of the country.</p>
<p>Sirleaf initially moved to Washington, D.C., and worked for the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>. In 1981 she moved to <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>, Kenya to serve as Vice President of the African Regional Office of <a href="/wiki/Citibank" title="Citibank">Citibank</a>. She resigned from Citibank in 1985 following her involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Liberian_general_election,_1985" title="Liberian general election, 1985">1985 election</a> in Liberia. She went to work for Equator Bank, a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/HSBC" title="HSBC">HSBC</a>.</p>
<p>In 1992, Sirleaf was appointed as the Director of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">United Nations Development Programme</a>'s Regional Bureau for Africa at the rank of Assistant Administrator and Assistant Secretary General (ASG). She resigned in 1997 in order to run for president in Liberia. During her time at the UN, she was one of the seven internationally eminent persons designated in 1999 by the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_African_Unity" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization of African Unity">Organization of African Unity</a> to investigate the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">Rwandan genocide</a>, one of the five Commission Chairs for the Inter-Congolese Dialogue, and one of two international experts selected by <a href="/wiki/UNIFEM" class="mw-redirect" title="UNIFEM">UNIFEM</a> to investigate and report on the effect of conflict on women and women's roles in peace building. She was the initial Chairperson of the <a href="/wiki/Open_Society_Initiative_for_West_Africa" title="Open Society Initiative for West Africa">Open Society Initiative for West Africa</a> (OSIWA) and a visiting Professor of Governance at the <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Institute_of_Management_and_Public_Administration" title="Ghana Institute of Management and Public Administration">Ghana Institute of Management and Public Administration</a> (GIMPA).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_career">Political career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Political career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1985_general_election">1985 general election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1985 general election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While working at Citibank, Sirleaf returned to Liberia in 1985 to run for <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_Liberia" title="Vice President of Liberia">Vice President</a> under <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Doe" title="Jackson Doe">Jackson Doe</a> on the ticket of the <a href="/wiki/Liberian_Action_Party" title="Liberian Action Party">Liberian Action Party</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Liberian_general_election,_1985" title="Liberian general election, 1985">1985 elections</a>. However, Sirleaf was placed under <a href="/wiki/House_arrest" title="House arrest">house arrest</a> in August 1985 and soon after sentenced to ten years in prison for <a href="/wiki/Sedition" title="Sedition">sedition</a>, as a consequence of a speech in which she insulted the members of the (Samuel) Doe regime. Following international calls for her release, Doe pardoned and released her in September. Due to government pressure, she was removed from the presidential ticket and instead ran for a <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Liberia" title="Senate of Liberia">Senate</a> seat in <a href="/wiki/Montserrado_County" title="Montserrado County">Montserrado County</a>.</p>
<p>In the 1985 elections, Doe and the <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_of_Liberia" title="National Democratic Party of Liberia">National Democratic Party</a> won the presidency and large majorities in both house. The elections were widely condemned as neither free nor fair. Sirleaf was declared the winner of her Senate race, but she refused to accept the seat in protest of the election fraud.</p>
<p>After an attempted coup against the Doe government by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Quiwonkpa" title="Thomas Quiwonkpa">Thomas Quiwonkpa</a> on 12 November 1985, Sirleaf was arrested and imprisoned again on 13 November by Doe's forces. Despite continuing to refuse to accept her seat in the Senate, she was released in July 1986. She secretly fled the country to the United States later that year.<sup id="cite_ref-Leaders_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaders-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1997_presidential_campaign">1997 presidential campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1997 presidential campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf_2005.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf_2005.jpg/175px-Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf_2005.jpg" width="175" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf_2005.jpg/263px-Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf_2005.jpg/350px-Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf_2005.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1012" data-file-height="1197" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf_2005.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sirleaf campaigning in Monrovia in 2005, shortly before she was elected.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/First_Liberian_Civil_War" title="First Liberian Civil War">First Liberian Civil War</a> in 1989, Sirleaf supported <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taylor_(Liberia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Taylor (Liberia)">Charles Taylor</a>'s rebellion against Doe. She helped raise money for the war and founded the <a href="/wiki/National_Patriotic_Front_of_Liberia" title="National Patriotic Front of Liberia">National Patriotic Front of Liberia</a> with Taylor and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Woewiyu" title="Tom Woewiyu">Tom Woewiyu</a>. Because of this, Doe's government recommended that Sirleaf be banned from politics in Liberia for 30 years.</p>
<p>But, she later opposed Taylor's handling of the war and his treatment of rival opposition leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Doe" title="Jackson Doe">Jackson Doe</a>. By 1996, the presence of <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">Economic Community of West African States</a> (ECOWAS) peacekeepers led to a cessation of hostilities. The nation held the <a href="/wiki/Liberian_general_election,_1997" title="Liberian general election, 1997">1997 general election</a>, which Sirleaf returned to Liberia to contest. She ran as the presidential candidate for the <a href="/wiki/Unity_Party_(Liberia)" title="Unity Party (Liberia)">Unity Party</a> and placed second in a controversial election, getting 25% of the vote to Charles Taylor's 75%. After controversy about the results and being accused of treason, Sirleaf left Liberia and went into exile in <a href="/wiki/Abidjan" title="Abidjan">Abidjan</a>, Ivory Coast.<sup id="cite_ref-Leaders_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaders-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2005_presidential_campaign">2005 presidential campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2005 presidential campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the end of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Liberian_Civil_War" title="Second Liberian Civil War">Second Liberian Civil War</a> and the establishment of a transitional government, Sirleaf was proposed as a possible candidate for chairman of the government. Ultimately, <a href="/wiki/Gyude_Bryant" title="Gyude Bryant">Gyude Bryant</a>, a political neutral, was chosen as chairman, while Sirleaf served as head of the Governance Reform Commission.</p>
<p>Sirleaf stood for president as the candidate of the Unity Party in the <a href="/wiki/Liberian_general_election,_2005" title="Liberian general election, 2005">2005 general election</a>. She placed second in the first round of voting behind <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">footballer</a> <a href="/wiki/George_Weah" title="George Weah">George Weah</a>. In the subsequent run-off election, Sirleaf earned 59% of the vote versus 40% for Weah, though Weah disputed the results. The announcement of the new leader was postponed until further election investigations were carried out. On 23 November 2005, Sirleaf was declared the winner of the Liberian election and confirmed as the country's next president.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Her inauguration, attended by many foreign dignitaries, including <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">United States Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Condoleezza_Rice" title="Condoleezza Rice">Condoleezza Rice</a> and <a href="/wiki/First_Lady_of_the_United_States" title="First Lady of the United States">First Lady</a> <a href="/wiki/Laura_Bush" title="Laura Bush">Laura Bush</a>, took place on 16 January 2006.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2011_presidential_campaign">2011 presidential campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2011 presidential campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawakkul_Karman_Leymah_Gbowee_Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_Nobel_Peace_Prize_2011_Harry_Wad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Tawakkul_Karman_Leymah_Gbowee_Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_Nobel_Peace_Prize_2011_Harry_Wad.jpg/350px-Tawakkul_Karman_Leymah_Gbowee_Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_Nobel_Peace_Prize_2011_Harry_Wad.jpg" width="350" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Tawakkul_Karman_Leymah_Gbowee_Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_Nobel_Peace_Prize_2011_Harry_Wad.jpg/525px-Tawakkul_Karman_Leymah_Gbowee_Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_Nobel_Peace_Prize_2011_Harry_Wad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Tawakkul_Karman_Leymah_Gbowee_Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_Nobel_Peace_Prize_2011_Harry_Wad.jpg/700px-Tawakkul_Karman_Leymah_Gbowee_Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_Nobel_Peace_Prize_2011_Harry_Wad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1300" data-file-height="830" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawakkul_Karman_Leymah_Gbowee_Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_Nobel_Peace_Prize_2011_Harry_Wad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Tawakkul_Karman" class="mw-redirect" title="Tawakkul Karman">Tawakkul Karman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leymah_Gbowee" title="Leymah Gbowee">Leymah Gbowee</a>, and Ellen Johnson Sirleaf display their awards during the presentation of the Nobel Peace Prize, 10 December 2011 (Photo:Harry Wad).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In January 2010, Sirleaf announced that she would run for a second term in office in the <a href="/wiki/Liberian_general_election,_2011" title="Liberian general election, 2011">2011 presidential election</a> while speaking to a joint session of the Legislature.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Opposition leaders noted that in doing so, she had broken a promise made during her 2005 campaign to only serve one term if elected.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Sirleaf was renominated as the Unity Party's presidential candidate at the party's national convention on 31 October 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Secterm_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Secterm-22">[22]</a></sup> That same day, Vice President <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Boakai" title="Joseph Boakai">Joseph Boakai</a> was nominated by Sirleaf and confirmed by the delegates as Sirleaf's running mate.<sup id="cite_ref-Secterm_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Secterm-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The awarding of the Nobel Peace Prize to Sirleaf four days prior to the election sparked criticism from opposition parties, with <a href="/wiki/Congress_for_Democratic_Change" title="Congress for Democratic Change">Congress for Democratic Change</a> candidate <a href="/wiki/Winston_Tubman" title="Winston Tubman">Winston Tubman</a> calling the award "undeserved" and "a political interference in our country's politics."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Sirleaf called the timing of the award a coincidence and avoided mentioning the award during the final days of campaigning.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sirleaf garnered 43.9% of the vote in the first round, more than any other candidate but short of the 50% needed to avoid a run-off. Tubman came in second with 32.7%, pitting him against Sirleaf in the second round.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Tubman called for a boycott of the run-off, claiming that the results of the first round had been fraudulent.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Sirleaf denied the allegations, and international observers reported that the first round election had been free, fair and transparent.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> As a result of the boycott, Sirleaf won the second round with 90.7% of the vote, though voter turnout significantly declined from the first round.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Following the election, Sirleaf announced the creation of a "national peace and reconciliation initiative," led by Nobel Peace Prize laureate <a href="/wiki/Leymah_Gbowee" title="Leymah Gbowee">Leymah Gbowee</a>, to address the country's divisions and begin "a national dialogue that would bring us together."<sup id="cite_ref-allow_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allow-29">[29]</a></sup> She took presidential oath for her second presidency on 16 January 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency">Presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policy">Domestic policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Domestic policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf3.jpg/220px-Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf3.jpg" width="220" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf3.jpg/330px-Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf3.jpg/440px-Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="515" data-file-height="595" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellen_Johnson-Sirleaf3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sirleaf at her inauguration in Monrovia.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A fire broke out at the Executive Mansion on 26 July 2006, seriously damaging the structure. An independent panel formed to investigate the incident ruled out arson, attributing the fire to an electrical malfunction.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Sirleaf's government called funding for the repair of the mansion a low priority in the face of more pressing needs, with Sirleaf transferring her office to the nearby Foreign Ministry building and choosing to live at her personal home in Monrovia.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 26 July 2007, Sirleaf celebrated Liberia's 160th Independence Day under the theme "Liberia at 160: Reclaiming the future." She took an unprecedented and symbolic move by asking 25-year-old Liberian activist <a href="/wiki/Kimmie_Weeks" title="Kimmie Weeks">Kimmie Weeks</a> to serve as National Orator for the celebrations, where Weeks called for the government to prioritize education and health care. A few days later, President Sirleaf issued an Executive Order making education free and compulsory for all elementary school aged children.</p>
<p>On 4 October 2010, Sirleaf signed into law a <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_information_legislation" class="mw-redirect" title="Freedom of information legislation">Freedom of Information bill</a>, the first legislation of its kind in West Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In recognition of this, she became the first sitting head of state to receive the Friend of the Media in Africa Award from The African Editor's Union.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 April 2011, Sirleaf told reporters that she planned to charge an opposition candidate with <a href="/wiki/Sedition" title="Sedition">sedition</a> for organizing a rally protesting corruption in the government. Her press secretary later clarified that the remark had been an <a href="/wiki/April_Fools%27_Day" title="April Fools' Day">April Fools' prank</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Debt_relief">Debt relief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Debt relief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>From the beginning of her presidency, Sirleaf vowed to make reduction of the <a href="/wiki/National_debt" class="mw-redirect" title="National debt">national debt</a>, which stood at approximately US$4.9&#160;billion in 2006, a top priority for her administration. The United States became the first country to grant debt relief to Liberia, waiving the full $391&#160;million owed to it by Liberia in early 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In September of that year, the <a href="/wiki/G8" class="mw-redirect" title="G8">G-8</a> headed by German <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany" title="Chancellor of Germany">Chancellor</a> <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Angela Merkel</a> provided $324.5&#160;million to paying off 60% of Liberia's debt to the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>, crediting their decision to the macroeconomic policies pursued by the Sirleaf administration.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2009, the government successfully wrote off an additional $1.2&#160;billion in foreign <a href="/wiki/Debt" title="Debt">commercial debt</a> in a deal that saw the government buy back the debt at a 97% discounted rate through financing provided by the <a href="/wiki/International_Development_Association" title="International Development Association">International Development Association</a>, Germany, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, the United States and the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Slash_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slash-40">[40]</a></sup> The discounted rate was the largest ever for a developing country.<sup id="cite_ref-Slash_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slash-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The country was deemed eligible to participate in the <a href="/wiki/Heavily_Indebted_Poor_Countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavily Indebted Poor Countries">Heavily Indebted Poor Countries</a> initiative in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In June 2010, the country reached the completion point of the HIPC initiative, qualifying it for relief from its entire external debt.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> That same month, the World Bank and IMF agreed to fund $1.5&#160;billion in writing off the Liberia's multilateral debt.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> On 16 September, the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Club" title="Paris Club">Paris Club</a> agreed to cancel $1.26&#160;billion, with independent bilateral creditors canceling an additional $107&#160;million, essentially writing off Liberia's remaining external debt.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Sirleaf vowed to prevent unsustainable borrowing in the future by restricting annual borrowing to 3% of GDP and limiting expenditure of all borrowed funds to one-off infrastructure projects.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Truth_and_Reconciliation_Commission">Truth and Reconciliation Commission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Truth and Reconciliation Commission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_gc2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_gc2008.jpg/200px-Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_gc2008.jpg" width="200" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_gc2008.jpg/300px-Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_gc2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_gc2008.jpg/400px-Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_gc2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1698" data-file-height="1290" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf_gc2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President Sirleaf addressing the 2008 General Conference of the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Worth,_Texas" title="Fort Worth, Texas">Fort Worth, Texas</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2006, Sirleaf established a <a href="/wiki/Truth_and_Reconciliation_Commission_(Liberia)" title="Truth and Reconciliation Commission (Liberia)">Truth and Reconciliation Commission</a> with a mandate to "promote national peace, security, unity and reconciliation" by investigating more than 20 years of civil conflict in the country.</p>
<p>In their final report, issued in June 2009, the TRC included Sirleaf in a list of 50 names of people that should be "specifically barred from holding public offices; elected or appointed for a period of thirty (30) years" for "being associated with former warring factions."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The proposed ban stemmed from her financial support of former President Taylor in the early years of the First Liberian Civil War.</p>
<p>On 26 July 2009, Sirleaf apologized to Liberia for supporting Charles Taylor, saying: "When the true nature of Mr. Taylor's intentions became known, there was no more impassioned critic or strong opponent to him in a democratic process" than she.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> On 28 August, the Legislature announced they must "consult our constituents for about a year" before deciding whether or not to implement the Commission's recommendations.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>During an appearance at the <a href="/wiki/Council_on_Foreign_Relations" title="Council on Foreign Relations">Council on Foreign Relations</a> in 2010, Sirleaf argued that the implementation of the TRC's recommended ban would unconstitutionally violate her right to <a href="/wiki/Due_process" title="Due process">due process</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CFR_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CFR-49">[49]</a></sup> In October 2010, the chairman of Sirleaf's Unity Party, <a href="/wiki/Varney_Sherman" title="Varney Sherman">Varney Sherman</a>, argued that implementation of the recommendation would be unconstitutional, as Article 21(a) of the Constitution prohibits <i><a href="/wiki/Ex_post_facto" class="mw-redirect" title="Ex post facto">ex post facto</a></i> laws, and Sirleaf had broken no law by financially supporting Taylor that imposed a ban from public office as a penalty.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Liberia" title="Supreme Court of Liberia">Supreme Court</a> ruled in <i>Williams v. Tah</i>, a case brought by another person recommended for being banned from public office in the TRC report, that the TRC's recommendation was an unconstitutional violation of the listed individuals' right to <a href="/wiki/Procedural_due_process" title="Procedural due process">procedural due process</a>, and that it would be unconstitutional for the government to implement the proposed bans.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gay_rights">Gay rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Gay rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Following a speech made by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">United States Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> in December 2011 that America's foreign aid would be used to promote the protection of gay rights,<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_-_homosexuality_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_-_homosexuality-52">[52]</a></sup> the issue of <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Liberia" title="LGBT rights in Liberia">LGBT rights</a> became a significant political topic in Liberia. According to <i><a href="/wiki/Guardian_(newspaper)" class="mw-redirect" title="Guardian (newspaper)">The Guardian</a></i>, "Since Clinton's remarks, Liberian newspapers have published numerous articles and editorials describing homosexuality as 'desecrating', 'abusive' and an 'abomination'."<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_-_homosexuality_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_-_homosexuality-52">[52]</a></sup> Liberian law made "<a href="/wiki/Sodomy" title="Sodomy">voluntary sodomy</a>" punishable by up to one year in prison, although it has not been used to prosecute anyone in several years.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2012, <a href="/wiki/Bong_County" title="Bong County">Bong County</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Jewel_Taylor" title="Jewel Taylor">Jewel Taylor</a> proposed a bill that would carry a term of ten years in prison for homosexual activity, while a similar bill was introduced in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_of_Liberia" title="House of Representatives of Liberia">House of Representatives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_-_homosexuality_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_-_homosexuality-52">[52]</a></sup> On 19 March, Sirleaf addressed the issue, saying that she would not repeal the current law but would also not sign into law either of the two proposed bills. Sirleaf added, "We like ourselves just the way we are [...] We've got certain traditional values in our society that we would like to preserve."<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_-_homosexuality_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_-_homosexuality-52">[52]</a></sup> According to Tiawan Gongloe, Liberia's former Solicitor General, "If she tried to decriminalise the [current anti-gay] law it would be political suicide."<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_-_homosexuality_52-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_-_homosexuality-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a letter to <i>The Guardian,</i> Sirleaf's press secretary challenged the portrayal of her remarks in the media saying that: "There currently exists no law referencing homosexuality in Liberia, and as such the President could not be defending a law on homosexuality. The President is on record as saying [...] that any law brought before her regarding homosexuality will be vetoed. This statement also applies to an initial attempt by two members of the Liberian legislature to introduce tougher laws targeting homosexuality." The letter added "the status quo in Liberia has been one of tolerance and no one has ever been prosecuted under that [current] law," and went on to hint at future possible liberalization stating that "the President thinks that with the unprecedented freedom of speech and expression Liberia enjoys today, our budding democracy will be strong enough to accommodate new ideas and debate both their value and Liberia's laws with openness, respect and independence."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> <i>The Guardian</i> published a correction to its story, "Nobel peace prize winner defends law criminalising homosexuality in Liberia was updated to restore material cut in the editing process. The restored material clarifies the stance that President Ellen Johnson Sirleaf is taking on laws concerning homosexuality in Liberia. That is: she refuses to dismantle the existing anti-sodomy law, while also saying she will refuse to sign two new bills that would toughen laws on homosexuality." The comments, letter, and clarification suggest that she considered the status quo for gay rights in Liberia to be one of <i>de facto</i> tolerance up until the recent controversy and would not support decriminalization of homosexuality, but also refuses to support further criminalisation of homosexual acts which was being attempted in Liberia and hints at future liberalization. This is a view she reaffirmed during an interview with Tony Blair.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Upon her election to office, Sirleaf made her first foreign trip as President to neighboring <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>, meeting with Ivorian President <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Gbagbo" title="Laurent Gbagbo">Laurent Gbagbo</a> in an attempt to repair relations between the two countries following Côte d'Ivoire's support of the <a href="/wiki/Movement_for_Democracy_in_Liberia" title="Movement for Democracy in Liberia">Movement for Democracy in Liberia</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Liberian_Civil_War" title="Second Liberian Civil War">Second Liberian Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%932011_Ivorian_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2010–2011 Ivorian crisis">2010–2011 Ivorian crisis</a>, Sirleaf, as chairperson of the <a href="/wiki/Mano_River_Union" title="Mano River Union">Mano River Union</a>, supported ECOWAS's recognition of Gbagbo's opponent, <a href="/wiki/Alassane_Ouattara" title="Alassane Ouattara">Alassane Ouattara</a>, as the winner of the <a href="/wiki/Ivorian_presidential_election,_2010" title="Ivorian presidential election, 2010">disputed presidential election</a>, but rejected calls for a military solution to the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sirleaf has forged close relations with the United States, Liberia's traditional ally. Following the establishment of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Africa_Command" title="United States Africa Command">United States Africa Command</a> (AFRICOM) by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_military" class="mw-redirect" title="United States military">United States military</a>, Sirleaf offered to allow the US to headquarter the new command in Liberia, the only African leader to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The command was eventually headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">Stuttgart</a>, Germany. On 15 March 2006, President Sirleaf addressed a joint meeting of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>, asking for American support to help her country "become a brilliant beacon, an example to Africa and the world of what love of liberty can achieve."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sirleaf has also strengthened relations with the People's Republic of China, reaffirming Liberia's commitment to the <a href="/wiki/One-China_policy" title="One-China policy">One-China policy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In return, China has contributed to Liberia's reconstruction, building several transmitters to extend the <a href="/wiki/Liberia_Broadcasting_System" title="Liberia Broadcasting System">Liberia Broadcasting System</a> nationwide and constructing a new campus for the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Liberia" title="University of Liberia">University of Liberia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sirleaf is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Women_World_Leaders" title="Council of Women World Leaders">Council of Women World Leaders</a>, an international network of current and former women presidents and prime ministers whose mission is to mobilize the highest-level women leaders globally for collective action on issues of critical importance to women and equitable development.</p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_civil_war" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Libyan civil war">2011 Libyan civil war</a>, Sirleaf added her voice to a chorus of calls from the international community for <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libyan</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Muammar_al-Gaddafi" class="mw-redirect" title="Muammar al-Gaddafi">Muammar al-Gaddafi</a> to cease the use of violence and tactics of political repression. However, she criticized the <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">international military intervention</a> in Libya, declaring that "violence does not help the process whichever way it comes".<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Her government later severed diplomatic ties with Libya, stating that "The Government took the decision after a careful review of the situation in Libya and determined that the Government of Colonel Gaddafi has lost the legitimacy to govern Libya."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 27 February 2015, President Sirleaf was expected to make a visit to U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, according to an official online statement from the Office of the <a href="/wiki/White_House_Press_Secretary" title="White House Press Secretary">White House Press Secretary</a>. Among other issues, they planned to discuss the hope to expeditiously close the recent 2013-2015 <a href="/wiki/Ebola_virus_epidemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ebola virus epidemic">Ebola virus epidemic</a>, which heavily affected <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a>, and other areas in West Africa (and beyond in other countries due to importation of cases for treatment and some new infections), down to an ideal of zero reported cases in Liberia and nearby areas in the near future, with continuing monitoring and reporting, care, support, and fiscal and professional assistance. They also planned to discuss how to sustain and rebuild the healthcare infrastructure and the country's other difficulties in the wake of the massive outbreak's morbidity and mortality toll and impact on the area, as well as review progress that had been made and efforts to continue it.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>

<p>Following her election in 2005, Sirleaf pledged to promote national reconciliation by bringing in opposition leaders into her administration.<sup id="cite_ref-profile_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-profile-66">[66]</a></sup> Opposition politicians who joined her initial administration included Minister of Transport Jeremiah Sulunteh, Minister of Education <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Korto" title="Joseph Korto">Joseph Korto</a>, and Ambassador to the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Barnes" title="Nathaniel Barnes">Nathaniel Barnes</a>. Sirleaf also appointed several women to high-level posts in her administration, with female ministers initially leading the Ministries of Finance, Law, Commerce and Industry, Gender and Development, and Youth and Sports.<sup id="cite_ref-appoint_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-appoint-67">[67]</a></sup> Sirleaf said that while she had planned on appointing an all-female cabinet, she had been unable to find qualified female candidates for every position.<sup id="cite_ref-CFR_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CFR-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Upon her inauguration, Sirleaf promised that she would impose a "zero tolerance" policy on corruption within the government.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Despite this, critics have argued that corruption remains rampant within Sirleaf's administration; Information Minister Lawrence Bropleh was sacked in 2008 over allegations that he had stolen more than $200,000 in state funds, while Internal Affairs Minister Ambullai Johnson, Sirleaf's brother, was dismissed in 2010 after the disappearance of funds for county development.<sup id="cite_ref-tarnish_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tarnish-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Sirleaf herself has acknowledged that corruption in government remains, noting that her zero tolerance policy was hampered by the need to pass major economic reforms through the Legislature, a goal that would have been impeded by significant anti-corruption legislation and prosecutions.<sup id="cite_ref-tarnish_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tarnish-69">[69]</a></sup> However, Sirleaf has rejected claims that she has failed to fight corruption, pointing to the establishment of the Liberian Anti-Corruption Commission and the restructuring of the General Auditing Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sirleaf dismissed her entire cabinet from office on 3 November 2010, promising to reassemble the cabinet in as short a time as possible.<sup id="cite_ref-dissolve_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dissolve-72">[72]</a></sup> She argued that the move was taken to give her administration a "clean slate" in preparation for the final year of her term, though critics argued that the move was aimed to bolster her chances at reelection by confronting corruption in her administration.<sup id="cite_ref-dissolve_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dissolve-72">[72]</a></sup> By early December 2010, Sirleaf had reconstituted her entire cabinet, replacing seven of her nineteen ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Upon the inauguration of Sirleaf, the entire Supreme Court bench, which had been selected as part of the transitional government in 2003, stepped down, leaving Sirleaf to fill all five seats on the Court. Sirleaf nominated <a href="/wiki/Johnnie_Lewis" title="Johnnie Lewis">Johnnie Lewis</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Yale_Law_School" title="Yale Law School">Yale Law School</a> graduate and former Circuit Court judge, for the office of <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_Liberia" title="Chief Justice of Liberia">Chief Justice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Lewis and three of Sirleaf's Associate Justice nominees, J. Emmanuel Wureh, Francis Korkpor and Gladys Johnson, were confirmed by the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Liberia" title="Senate of Liberia">Senate</a> on 2 March 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Sirleaf's nomination of Kabineh Ja'neh, a former leader in the rebel <a href="/wiki/Liberians_United_for_Reconciliation_and_Democracy" title="Liberians United for Reconciliation and Democracy">LURD</a> movement, as Associate Justice received criticism from the opposition <a href="/wiki/Congress_for_Democratic_Change" title="Congress for Democratic Change">Congress for Democratic Change</a> due to concerns over Ja'neh's human rights record during the civil war, and Ja'neh was not confirmed until 9 May.<sup id="cite_ref-appoint_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-appoint-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the death of Justice Wureh in July 2006, Sirleaf nominated Christiana Tah, a deputy minister at the Justice Ministry, to fill his seat.<sup id="cite_ref-void_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-void-77">[77]</a></sup> However, the Senate later rejected Tah's nomination, leading Sirleaf to nominate her Minister of Youth and Sports, Jamesetta Howard Wolokollie, who was confirmed.<sup id="cite_ref-void_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-void-77">[77]</a></sup> Justice Johnson retired from the Court on 26 March 2011 after reaching the constitutionally mandated retirement age of seventy.<sup id="cite_ref-void_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-void-77">[77]</a></sup> Sirleaf nominated Phillip A. Z. Banks, her former Minister of Justice and Chairman of the Law Reform Commission, to replace Johnson in August 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Banks was confirmed by the Senate on 20 August 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_image">International image</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: International image">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> magazine named Sirleaf as the 51st <a href="/wiki/The_World%27s_100_Most_Powerful_Women" class="mw-redirect" title="The World's 100 Most Powerful Women">most powerful woman in the world</a> in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In 2010, <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i> listed her as one of the ten best leaders in the world, while <i>Time</i> counted her among the top ten female leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> That same year, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> called her "arguably the best president the country has ever had."<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In 2010, Sirleaf released her first book, <i>This Child Will Be Great: Memoir of a Remarkable Life by Africa's First Woman President</i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1956, Sirleaf married James Sirleaf, whom she later divorced.<sup id="cite_ref-bergner_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bergner-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (September 2011)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> She grew up as a <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a>, but later joined her husband's <a href="/wiki/Methodist" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist">Methodist</a> faith.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Sirleaf is the mother of four sons,<sup id="cite_ref-bergner_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bergner-14">[14]</a></sup> and she has ten grandchildren. She is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Kappa_Alpha" title="Alpha Kappa Alpha">Alpha Kappa Alpha</a> sorority and an honorary member of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Links,_Incorporated&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Links, Incorporated (page does not exist)">Links, Incorporated</a>. She is the aunt to American actress/comic <a href="/wiki/Retta" title="Retta">Retta</a> (born Marietta Sirleaf<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>), best known for her role as Donna Meagle on the <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> <a href="/wiki/Television_comedy" title="Television comedy">comedy</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Parks_and_Recreation" title="Parks and Recreation">Parks and Recreation</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-hollywoodreporter.com_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hollywoodreporter.com-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hollywoodreporter.com_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hollywoodreporter.com-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among her relatives, several serve in the Liberian government. Robert Sirleaf served as head of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Oil_Company_of_Liberia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="National Oil Company of Liberia (page does not exist)">National Oil Company of Liberia</a>, Charles Sirleaf in a senior position at the <a href="/wiki/Central_Bank_of_Liberia" title="Central Bank of Liberia">Central Bank of Liberia</a> and Fombah Sirleaf serving as head of the National Security Agency. <sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Other members of the Sirleaf family are serving in other positions in government.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards">Awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Recipient of the 1988 <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Institute" title="Roosevelt Institute">Roosevelt Institute</a> Freedom of Speech Award<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ralph_Bunche" title="Ralph Bunche">Ralph Bunche</a> International Leadership Award</li>
<li>Grand commander Star of Africa Redemption of Liberia</li>
<li><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Commandeur de l'Ordre du Togo</span> (Commander of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Mono" title="Order of Mono">Order of Mono</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li>2006 Common Ground Award recipient, <a href="/wiki/Search_for_Common_Ground" title="Search for Common Ground">Search for Common Ground</a><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></li>
<li>2006 Laureate of the Africa Prize for Leadership for the Sustainable End of Hunger, <a href="/wiki/The_Hunger_Project" title="The Hunger Project">The Hunger Project</a><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li>2006 Distinguished Fellow, Claus M. Halle Institute for Global Learning, Emory University<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>2006 Awarded Honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Laws">Doctor of Laws</a> from <a href="/wiki/Marquette_University" title="Marquette University">Marquette University</a><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li>2006 David Rockefeller Bridging Leadership Award from <a href="/wiki/Synergos" title="Synergos">Synergos</a><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li>2007 <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a>, the highest civilian award given by the United States, awarded to Sirleaf by U.S. President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> on 5 November 2007<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>2008 Awarded Honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Laws">Doctor of Laws</a> degree from <a href="/wiki/Indiana_University_(Bloomington)" class="mw-redirect" title="Indiana University (Bloomington)">Indiana University</a><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>; and <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li>2009 Awarded the <a href="/wiki/EITI" class="mw-redirect" title="EITI">EITI</a> Award for "the rapid progress the country has made towards implementation of the EITI"<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></li>
<li>2009 Awarded Honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Humane_Letters" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Humane Letters">Doctor of Humane Letters</a> degree from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tampa" title="University of Tampa">University of Tampa</a><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></li>
<li>2010 Awarded Honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Laws">Doctor of Laws</a> degree from <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rutgers,_The_State_University_of_New_Jersey" class="mw-redirect" title="Rutgers, The State University of New Jersey">Rutgers, The State University of New Jersey</a><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>2010 Friend of the Media in Africa Award from The African Editor's Union<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li>2011 Awarded Honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Laws">Doctor of Laws</a> degree from <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></li>
<li>2011 African Gender Award<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>2011 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a><sup id="cite_ref-nobel_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li>2012 <a href="/wiki/Indira_Gandhi_Prize" title="Indira Gandhi Prize">Indira Gandhi Prize</a> for Peace, Disarmament and Development<sup id="cite_ref-news.biharprabha.com_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.biharprabha.com-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li>As of 2014, she is listed as the 70th most powerful woman in the world by <i>Forbes</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes14_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes14-4">[4]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellen_Johnson_Sirleaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Torild_Skard" title="Torild Skard">Skard, Torild</a> (2014). <li><span style="color:darkgrey"><span style="font-size:90%;">1967</span></span></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1968</span> <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Cassin" title="René Cassin">René Cassin</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1969</span> <a href="/wiki/International_Labour_Organization" title="International Labour Organization">International Labour Organization</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1970</span> <a href="/wiki/Norman_Borlaug" title="Norman Borlaug">Norman Borlaug</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1971</span> <a href="/wiki/Willy_Brandt" title="Willy Brandt">Willy Brandt</a></li>

<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1974</span> <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_MacBride" title="Seán MacBride">Seán MacBride</a>&#160;/ <a href="/wiki/Eisaku_Sat%C5%8D" title="Eisaku Satō">Eisaku Satō</a></li>
